<p><i><b>StarCraft II: Wings of Liberty</b></i> is a <a href="/wiki/Military_science_fiction" title="Military science fiction">military science fiction</a> <a href="/wiki/Real-time_strategy" title="Real-time strategy">real-time strategy</a> <a href="/wiki/Video_game" title="Video game">video game</a> developed and published by <a href="/wiki/Blizzard_Entertainment" title="Blizzard Entertainment">Blizzard Entertainment</a>. It was released worldwide in July 2010 for <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Windows" title="Microsoft Windows">Microsoft Windows</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mac_OS_X" title="Mac OS X">Mac OS X</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Sequel" title="Sequel">sequel</a> to the award-winning 1998 video game <i><a href="/wiki/StarCraft_(video_game)" title="StarCraft (video game)">StarCraft</a></i> and its expansion set <i><a href="/wiki/StarCraft:_Brood_War" title="StarCraft: Brood War">Brood War</a></i>, the game is split into three installments: the base game with the subtitle <i>Wings of Liberty</i>, an <a href="/wiki/Expansion_pack" title="Expansion pack">expansion pack</a> <i><a href="/wiki/StarCraft_II:_Heart_of_the_Swarm" title="StarCraft II: Heart of the Swarm">Heart of the Swarm</a></i>, and a stand-alone expansion pack <i><a href="/wiki/StarCraft_II:_Legacy_of_the_Void" title="StarCraft II: Legacy of the Void">Legacy of the Void</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StarCraft_II_split_into_trilogy_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-StarCraft_II_split_into_trilogy-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>The game revolves around three species: the <a href="/wiki/Races_of_StarCraft#Terrans" title="Races of StarCraft">Terrans</a>, human exiles from Earth; the <a href="/wiki/Races_of_StarCraft#Zerg" title="Races of StarCraft">Zerg</a>, a super-species of assimilated life forms;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Races_of_StarCraft#Protoss" title="Races of StarCraft">Protoss</a>, a technologically advanced species with vast <a href="/wiki/Psionics" title="Psionics">mental</a> powers. <i>Wings of Liberty</i> focuses on the Terrans, while the expansions <i>Heart of the Swarm</i> and <i>Legacy of the Void</i> focus on the Zerg and Protoss, respectively. The game is set four years after the events of 1998's <i><a href="/wiki/StarCraft:_Brood_War" title="StarCraft: Brood War">StarCraft: Brood War</a></i>, and follows the exploits of <a href="/wiki/Jim_Raynor" title="Jim Raynor">Jim Raynor</a> as he leads an insurgent group against the autocratic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ara_Tribe#Terrans" title="Ara Tribe">Terran Dominion</a>. The game includes both new and returning characters and locations from the original game.</p>
<p>The game was met with very positive reviews from critics, receiving an aggregated score of 93% from <a href="/wiki/Metacritic" title="Metacritic">Metacritic</a>. Similar to its predecessor, <i>StarCraft II</i> was praised for its engaging gameplay, as well as its introduction of new features and improved storytelling. The game was criticized for lacking features that existed in the original <i>StarCraft</i> game including <a href="/wiki/Local_area_network" title="Local area network">LAN</a> play and the ability to switch between multiplayer regions. At the time of its release, <i>StarCraft II</i> became the fastest-selling <a href="/wiki/Real-time_strategy" title="Real-time strategy">real-time strategy</a> game of all time, with over three million copies sold worldwide in the first month.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3-million_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-3-million-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Gameplay">Gameplay</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=StarCraft_II:_Wings_of_Liberty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Gameplay">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hyperion_bridge_(StarCraft).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="360" data-file-width="640" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/64/Hyperion_bridge_%28StarCraft%29.jpg/220px-Hyperion_bridge_%28StarCraft%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/64/Hyperion_bridge_%28StarCraft%29.jpg/330px-Hyperion_bridge_%28StarCraft%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/64/Hyperion_bridge_%28StarCraft%29.jpg/440px-Hyperion_bridge_%28StarCraft%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hyperion_bridge_(StarCraft).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The new Terran briefing system allows the player to explore the inside of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_battleships_and_battlecruisers" title="Space battleships and battlecruisers">battlecruiser</a> <i>Hyperion</i>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i>StarCraft II</i> features the return of the three species from the original game: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protoss" title="Protoss">Protoss</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terran_Marine" title="Terran Marine">Terran</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zerg" title="Zerg">Zerg</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-officialfaq_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-officialfaq-1">[1]</a></sup> In the Terran campaign, the original <i>StarCraft</i> briefing room is replaced with an interactive version of the battlecruiser <i>Hyperion</i>, with <a href="/wiki/Jim_Raynor" title="Jim Raynor">Jim Raynor</a>, now a bitter and hard-drinking <a href="/wiki/Mercenary" title="Mercenary">mercenary</a> captain, as the central character. In a departure from previous Blizzard games, the campaign is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Linearity_(computer_and_video_games)" title="Linearity (computer and video games)">non-linear</a>, with Raynor taking jobs for money and using that money to buy additional units and upgrades. Although each play through will vary, the end result remains consistent, keeping the <a href="/wiki/Plot_(narrative)" title="Plot (narrative)">storyline</a> linear. Blizzard's Vice President <a href="/wiki/Rob_Pardo" title="Rob Pardo">Rob Pardo</a> stressed that each campaign will function very differently.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gamespotpardo_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-gamespotpardo-10">[10]</a></sup> The Terran campaign, <i>Wings of Liberty</i>, places players in a mercenary-style campaign, as Terran rebel group Raynor's Raiders raise funds by taking assignments from outside groups. The second release, <i>Heart of the Swarm</i>, is Zerg-focused and has <a href="/wiki/Role-playing_game" title="Role-playing game">role-playing</a> elements. <a href="/wiki/Sarah_Kerrigan" title="Sarah Kerrigan">Kerrigan</a> is the focus of the campaign, and the story revolves around the possibility of her redemption. The Protoss-themed <i>Legacy of the Void</i> will be the final expansion, with the Protoss Artanis attempting to reunite the Protoss tribes in order to stop Amon, a fallen Xel'Naga.</p>
<p><i>Wings of Liberty</i> has 29 playable campaign missions, but only 26 of them are playable in a single playthrough since three missions are choice-related alternates. There is one secret mission named "Piercing the Shroud", which can be unlocked on the "Media Blitz" mission, by destroying a Science Facility in a corner of the map. There is also a series of four missions in which the player plays as the Protoss in reliving the memory of Zeratul.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1upWatts_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-1upWatts-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1upNqyuen_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-1upNqyuen-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <i>Wings of Liberty</i> campaign contains several missions with unique features, such as lava that floods the battlefield every five minutes, forcing the player to move their units to high ground before they're destroyed. In another mission, enemy units will only attack the player at night, forcing the player into a form of siege warfare. Finally, in one mission, the player must use a single unit to influence the tide of an AI-controlled battle. The single player missions are highly customizable and are featured in the <i>StarCraft II</i> Community Zone. Between missions, players can choose units, buildings, and upgrades that are not available in the multiplayer missions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shack090701_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-shack090701-13">[13]</a></sup> A major new addition to the map-making community will be the <i>StarCraft II</i> Marketplace, where high quality maps will be sold for a small fee as "premium maps" over Battle.net. The mode of payment has not yet been announced. Lead Designer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dustin_Browder" title="Dustin Browder">Dustin Browder</a> has mentioned that even maps like player-created <a href="/wiki/Defense_of_the_Ancients" title="Defense of the Ancients">Defense of the Ancients</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/Warcraft_III:_Reign_of_Chaos" title="Warcraft III: Reign of Chaos">Warcraft III</a></i> would not meet the quality requirements to be branded as a premium map.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SC2Marketplace_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-SC2Marketplace-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>Wings of Liberty</i> features approximately the same number of units as the original <i>StarCraft</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gamespot5_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-gamespot5-15">[15]</a></sup> Some units from the original game have returned, some featuring new upgrades and abilities. For example, the Protoss Zealot, a melee unit from the original game, now has the researchable ability to dash forward and quickly reach nearby enemies as a refinement of its speed upgrade from the original. Other units have been replaced or removed entirely: for example, the Scout, a Protoss fighter craft present in the original, has been replaced by the Phoenix.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-protoss_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-protoss-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1upmielke_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-1upmielke-17">[17]</a></sup> Other changes to unit design have been inspired by story events in <i>StarCraft</i> and its expansion, <i><a href="/wiki/StarCraft:_Brood_War" title="StarCraft: Brood War">StarCraft: Brood War</a></i>, replacing old units with new or renamed versions which sport different attributes and abilities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-protoss_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-protoss-16">[16]</a></sup> Units in <i>StarCraft II</i> have new abilities, compared to the original, that encourage more complex interaction with the game environment. Among these are the inclusion of units that can traverse varying levels of terrain,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-video_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-video-18">[18]</a></sup> or have the ability to teleport short distances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-protoss_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-protoss-16">[16]</a></sup> Some Protoss units can be warped into pylon-powered areas using the Warp Gate, a slight modification of an existing building called the Gateway.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-protoss_16-3"><a href="#cite_note-protoss-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pcgamer07_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-pcgamer07-19">[19]</a></sup> <i>StarCraft II</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s campaign also has exclusive units that are only playable in the campaign and not in the regular multiplayer mode, though they are available for custom maps. These mostly consist of units that have been scrapped from development such as the Terran Diamondback as well as various returning units from the original <i>StarCraft</i> such as the Terran Wraith and Goliath. The campaign also features hirable mercenaries, modified versions of certain units with enhanced attributes such as health or damage that become available for hire once the standard unit is unlocked.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Q.26A_BT_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Q.26A_BT-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Editor">Editor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=StarCraft_II:_Wings_of_Liberty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Editor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <i>StarCraft II</i> Editor is a campaign, map, and mod editor. It is far more sophisticated than <i>StarCraft</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s StarEdit and <i><a href="/wiki/Warcraft_III:_Reign_of_Chaos" title="Warcraft III: Reign of Chaos">Warcraft III</a></i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s World Editor for creating custom maps and campaigns, and it is the first editor by Blizzard to feature built-in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mod_(computer_gaming)" title="Mod (computer gaming)">mod</a> creation and usage support. Updated art and data from the original <i>StarCraft</i> that were not used, along with models and data that were scrapped during the development process (including those made as <a href="/wiki/April_Fools%27_Day" title="April Fools' Day">April Fools</a> jokes) will be available in the editor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gamespot6_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-gamespot6-21">[21]</a></sup> Unlike previous editors made by Blizzard, it is the first to have Internet connectivity features such as map publishing, retrieval, and online activation of the editor client. Lead Producer Chris Sigaty has stated that the editor gives players the ability to create RPG, Hero-type units and structures resembling those from <i>WarCraft III</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> At <a href="/wiki/BlizzCon" title="BlizzCon">BlizzCon</a> 2009, Blizzard demonstrated a build of the <i>StarCraft II</i> Editor showcasing its capabilities, such as the ability to customize the <a href="/wiki/Graphical_user_interface" title="Graphical user interface">user interface</a> to include features such as the Item system from <i>Warcraft III</i>. The final build includes a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Third-person_view" title="Third-person view">third-person</a> style perspective for missions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>The editor was available for the first time during the phase 1 beta testing of <i>StarCraft II</i>, when it came with a patch. With the start of phase 2, the editor was updated. At present, there is a large map-making community using the editor, creating first-person shooter games, RPGs, <a href="/wiki/Tower_defense" title="Tower defense">tower defense</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Multiplayer_online_battle_arena" title="Multiplayer online battle arena">AoS</a>-type games. The map database is constantly being updated as more map makers create mods for the community. The new editor also changes the way maps are distributed: rather than hosting games using local map files, users now create and join games using maps that have been published to Battle.net. map or mod uploads are limited to a total of 50 <a href="/wiki/Megabyte" title="Megabyte">MB</a> of storage, divided between ten files at most, with no file being larger than 10 MB. Although the <i>StarCraft II</i> Editor offers more features than the original <i>StarCraft</i> Editor in terms of game customization, there are concerns that the publishing limitations of Battle.net will not allow for large-scale custom maps or extensive map availability unless there is an external map publishing tool.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>On Friday, November 4th, 2016 Alphabet's DeepMind branch announced the collaboration of Blizzard to create "a useful testing platform for the wider AI research community."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Synopsis">Synopsis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=StarCraft_II:_Wings_of_Liberty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Synopsis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=StarCraft_II:_Wings_of_Liberty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Baneling_attack_(StarCraft).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="281" data-file-width="375" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/cc/Baneling_attack_%28StarCraft%29.jpg/220px-Baneling_attack_%28StarCraft%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/cc/Baneling_attack_%28StarCraft%29.jpg/330px-Baneling_attack_%28StarCraft%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/c/cc/Baneling_attack_%28StarCraft%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Baneling_attack_(StarCraft).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A Zerg colony gathering resources and expanding its military.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The campaign storyline of <i>StarCraft II</i> takes place four years after <i><a href="/wiki/StarCraft:_Brood_War" title="StarCraft: Brood War">StarCraft: Brood War</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gamespot3_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-gamespot3-27">[27]</a></sup> and features the return of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zeratul" title="Zeratul">Zeratul</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arcturus_Mengsk" title="Arcturus Mengsk">Arcturus Mengsk</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artanis_(StarCraft)" title="Artanis (StarCraft)">Artanis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sarah_Kerrigan" title="Sarah Kerrigan">Sarah Kerrigan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jim_Raynor" title="Jim Raynor">Jim Raynor</a>. It also features new characters such as Rory Swann and Tychus Findlay. In <i>StarCraft II</i>, players revisit familiar worlds, like Char, Mar Sara, and Tarsonis, as well as new locations, such as the jungle planet Bel'Shir and New Folsom. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xel%27Naga" title="Xel'Naga">Xel'Naga</a>, an ancient space-faring race responsible for creating the Protoss and the Zerg, also play a major role in the story.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gamespotpardo_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-gamespotpardo-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the conclusion of <i>Brood War</i>, Kerrigan and her Zerg forces became the dominant faction in the Koprulu Sector, having annihilated the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Judicator_caste" title="Judicator caste">United Earth Directorate</a>'s Expeditionary Force, defeated the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ara_Tribe#Terrans" title="Ara Tribe">Terran Dominion</a>, and invaded the Protoss homeworld of Aiur. However, after the conclusion of <i>Brood War</i>, Kerrigan retreats to Char, despite having more than enough power to crush all remaining resistance in the Koprulu Sector. In the four years leading up to the events of <i>StarCraft II</i>, she has not been seen or heard from by any of the other characters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gamespot4_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-gamespot4-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>Arcturus Mengsk has been left to rebuild the Dominion, and is consolidating his power while fending off harassment from rival Terran groups. Mengsk has become power-hungry, declaring Jim Raynor an outlaw and showing little desire to protect or aid the colonies under his jurisdiction. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Valerian_Mengsk" title="Valerian Mengsk">Valerian Mengsk</a>, a character introduced in the novel <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/StarCraft_Uprising" title="StarCraft Uprising">Firstborn</a></i>, will play an important role in Dominion politics, due to his position as heir apparent to the throne. Meanwhile, Jim Raynor, whose role in the events of <i>StarCraft</i> and <i>Brood War</i> has been marginalized by the media under the Dominion's control, has become a mercenary who spends his free time drinking in Joeyray's Bar. <a href="/wiki/Chris_Metzen" title="Chris Metzen">Chris Metzen</a>, Vice President of Creative Development at Blizzard, has emphasized that by the events of <i>StarCraft II</i>, Raynor has become jaded and embittered by the way he was used and betrayed by Arcturus Mengsk. Other new characters to the series include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tychus_Findlay" title="Tychus Findlay">Tychus Findlay</a>, an ex-convict and <a href="/wiki/Space_marine" title="Space marine">marine</a> who becomes a member of Raynor's crew, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Matt_Horner" title="Matt Horner">Matt Horner</a>, Raynor's second in command, a character originally featured in the novel <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/StarCraft_Uprising" title="StarCraft Uprising">Queen of Blades</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gamespot4_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-gamespot4-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>Following the fall of Aiur and the death of the Dark Templar matriarch <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Raszagal" title="Raszagal">Raszagal</a>, the Protoss have retreated to the Dark Templar homeworld of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Koprulu_Sector#Shakuras" title="Koprulu Sector">Shakuras</a>. There, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artanis_(StarCraft)" title="Artanis (StarCraft)">Artanis</a>, a former student of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tassadar" title="Tassadar">Tassadar</a>, is trying to unify the Khalai Protoss and the Dark Templar, who have nearly separated into warring tribes as a result of centuries of distrust. Zeratul, tormented over the murder of his matriarch, has disappeared to search for clues to the meaning of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Samir_Duran" title="Samir Duran">Samir Duran</a>'s cryptic statements regarding the Protoss/Zerg hybrids in <i>Brood War's</i> secret mission "Dark Origin".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gamespot4_28-2"><a href="#cite_note-gamespot4-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Plot">Plot</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=StarCraft_II:_Wings_of_Liberty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Plot">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/StarCraft_II:_Heart_of_the_Swarm#Plot" title="StarCraft II: Heart of the Swarm">Plot of Starcraft II: Heart of the Swarm</a> and <a href="/wiki/StarCraft_II:_Legacy_of_the_Void#Plot" title="StarCraft II: Legacy of the Void">Plot of Starcraft II: Legacy of the Void</a></div>
<p>Four years after the Brood War, the Dominion is once again the dominant Terran power in the Koprulu sector. News reports reveal that in the four years since the end of the Brood Wars, the standing Dominion military forces have been reduced and defense budget has instead been diverted to hunting down rebel forces that operate against the Dominion. For reasons unknown, <a href="/wiki/Sarah_Kerrigan" title="Sarah Kerrigan">Kerrigan</a> gathered the swarm at Char and then vanished from sight. With the Zerg gone, the Protoss have once again taken a passive role in the galaxy. <a href="/wiki/Jim_Raynor" title="Jim Raynor">Jim Raynor</a> has formed a revolutionary group named Raynor's Raiders in order to overthrow Dominion Emperor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arcturus_Mengsk" title="Arcturus Mengsk">Arcturus Mengsk</a>. On Mar Sara, Raynor meets with an old comrade, Tychus Findlay. Together, they liberate the local population from Dominion control and also discover a component of a mysterious Xel'Naga artifact. As the Zerg begin to overrun Mar Sara, Raynor arranges an evacuation to his battlecruiser, the <i>Hyperion</i>, captained by Matt Horner, Raynor's second-in-command.</p>
<p>With Tychus acting as the middleman, the Raiders embark on a series of missions to find the remaining pieces of the Xel'Naga artifact, which they sell to the enigmatic Moebius Foundation in order to fund their revolution. Along the way, they meet with Gabriel Tosh, a rogue Dominion psychic assassin known as a Spectre, and Ariel Hanson, a researcher on the Zerg and leader of a small farming colony. The Raiders perform missions to help Tosh procure the raw materials to train new Spectres as well as to aid Hanson as she attempts to secure her colonists who are caught between the Zerg infesting their planets and the Protoss attempting to eradicate the infestation. Horner also arranges a series of missions to undermine Mengsk, recovering intelligence information about his war crimes and broadcasting them across the Dominion. Finally, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zeratul" title="Zeratul">Zeratul</a> sneaks aboard the <i>Hyperion</i> to deliver a psychic crystal that allows Raynor to share visions involving an ominous prophecy where Zerg-Protoss hybrids and an enslaved Zerg swarm wipe out Humanity and the Protoss. The vision reveals that only Kerrigan has the power to prevent the eradication of all life in the sector and beyond.</p>
<p>After collecting more artifact pieces, Raynor's forces encounter Dominion battleships at the pre-agreed Moebius Foundation rendezvous point. The Moebius Foundation is revealed to be under the control of Valerian Mengsk, Arcturus' son. Valerian, intending to show himself as a worthy successor to his father, asks Raynor to help him invade Char and use the artifact to restore Kerrigan's humanity, thus weakening the Zerg. To the initial dismay of the crew, Raynor agrees. With Valerian's aid, Raynor recovers the final artifact piece, and the Raiders and Dominion invade Char. The Dominion fleet is devastated by the heavy Zerg defenses, but Raynor secures a foothold on Char and rendezvouses with Dominion forces led by Horace Warfield, a decorated Dominion general. Warfield is later injured and appoints Raynor commander of surviving Dominion forces as well. The combined forces of the Raiders and Dominion military push towards the main Hive Cluster of the planet, protecting the artifact as it charges to full power, and the artifact eventually destroys all Zerg within its blast radius. Raynor's team finds Kerrigan restored to human form; however, Tychus reveals that he made a deal with Arcturus Mengsk, trading Kerrigan's life for his own freedom. Raynor defends Kerrigan from Tychus' attempt to kill her, fatally shooting Tychus in the process. The closing scene shows Raynor carrying Sarah Kerrigan out of the main hive in his arms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cast">Cast</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=StarCraft_II:_Wings_of_Liberty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Cast">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English language</a> version of <i>StarCraft II</i> has Robert Clotworthy and James Harper reprising their roles from <i>StarCraft</i> as the voices of Jim Raynor and Arcturus Mengsk. Notable absences included <a href="/wiki/Tricia_Helfer" title="Tricia Helfer">Tricia Helfer</a> replacing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glynnis_Talken" title="Glynnis Talken">Glynnis Talken</a> as Kerrigan, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Dorn" title="Michael Dorn">Michael Dorn</a> replacing <a href="/wiki/Michael_Gough_(voice_actor)" title="Michael Gough (voice actor)">Michael Gough</a> as Tassadar, and <a href="/wiki/Fred_Tatasciore" title="Fred Tatasciore">Fred Tatasciore</a> filling in for the late <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Jack_Ritschel&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Jack Ritschel (page does not exist)">Jack Ritschel</a> as Zeratul. The voice director for the game was <a href="/wiki/Andrea_Romano" title="Andrea Romano">Andrea Romano</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blizzcon2009lore_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blizzcon2009lore-30">[30]</a></sup> Over 58 voice actors were hired for the game, some of whom voiced multiple characters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Development">Development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=StarCraft_II:_Wings_of_Liberty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The development of <i>StarCraft II</i> was announced on May 19, 2007, at the <a href="/wiki/Blizzard_Worldwide_Invitational" title="Blizzard Worldwide Invitational">Blizzard Worldwide Invitational</a> in <a href="/wiki/Seoul" title="Seoul">Seoul</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ign1_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-ign1-32">[32]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Rob_Pardo" title="Rob Pardo">Rob Pardo</a> and Chris Sigaty, development on the game, though initially delayed for a year by the temporary reassignment of Blizzard's resources to <i><a href="/wiki/World_of_Warcraft" title="World of Warcraft">World of Warcraft</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> began in 2003, shortly after <i><a href="/wiki/Warcraft_III:_The_Frozen_Throne" title="Warcraft III: The Frozen Throne">Warcraft III: The Frozen Throne</a></i> was released.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> <i>StarCraft II</i> supports the <a href="/wiki/DirectX" title="DirectX">DirectX 9</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pixel_shader" title="Pixel shader">Pixel shader</a> 2.0) software, and it is also fully compatible with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DirectX_10" title="DirectX 10">DirectX 10</a> as well. The development team had decided not to add exclusive DirectX 10 graphic effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-officialfaq_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-officialfaq-1">[1]</a></sup> The Mac version uses <a href="/wiki/OpenGL" title="OpenGL">OpenGL</a>. The game previously featured the <a href="/wiki/Havok_(software)" title="Havok (software)">Havok</a> <a href="/wiki/Physics_engine" title="Physics engine">physics engine</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-officialfaq_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-officialfaq-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> which allowed for more realistic environmental elements such as "debris rolling down a ramp"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ign1_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-ign1-32">[32]</a></sup> which has since been replaced for a custom physics engine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Additionally, there are plans to implement <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/VoIP" title="VoIP">VoIP</a> into the game.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the June 2008 Blizzard Worldwide Invitational, Blizzard Executive Vice President Rob Pardo announced that development of the single-player campaign was approximately one-third complete,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> as well as that <i>Wings of Liberty</i> would be followed up by two expansion packs – <i><a href="/wiki/StarCraft_II:_Heart_of_the_Swarm" title="StarCraft II: Heart of the Swarm">StarCraft II: Heart of the Swarm</a></i>, which would focus around the Zerg and <i><a href="/wiki/StarCraft_II:_Legacy_of_the_Void" title="StarCraft II: Legacy of the Void">StarCraft II: Legacy of the Void</a></i>, which would focus around the Protoss.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StarCraft_II_split_into_trilogy_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-StarCraft_II_split_into_trilogy-7">[7]</a></sup> On February 25, 2009, Blizzard announced the Blizzard Theme Park Contest, where prizes would include two beta keys for <i>StarCraft II</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> The updated news and updates page of Battle.net for <i>Warcraft III: The Frozen Throne</i> stated that the top 20 players from each realm was to be given a <i>StarCraft II</i> beta key.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>Blizzard posted a release date for the game's first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Open_beta" title="Open beta">beta</a> of Summer 2009, but failed to release a beta during that time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bnet1_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-bnet1-41">[41]</a></sup> Since May 6, 2009, it was possible to sign up for the beta phase of the game.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morhaine_release2_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Morhaine_release2-44">[44]</a></sup> In November 2009, the game's producer Chris Sigaty confirmed there would be no public beta for the game taking place in 2009 but assured fans that it would happen next year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> In February 2010, Blizzard CEO Mike Morhaime announced that a closed beta would open later that month.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> On February 17, 2010, <i>StarCraft II</i> began closed beta testing, as promised.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> The beta was expected to last for 3–5 months. Beta keys for the initial release were sold on <a href="/wiki/EBay" title="EBay">eBay</a> for prices as high as $400.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Blizzard also released a map editor for the beta as part of Patch 9.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> According to the company, they had planned to release a major content patch towards the end of beta testing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blizzcast_13_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blizzcast_13-50">[50]</a></sup> As of July 23, 2010, eighteen patches had been released for the beta (only seventeen on European servers),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> including a patch which provided access to the <i>Galaxy</i> map editor. On May 12, 2010 Blizzard released the beta client for computers running <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mac_OS_X" title="Mac OS X">Mac OS X</a>, for the users who had signed up. On May 17, 2010, Blizzard announced that the first phase of the beta test would be coming to an end in all regions on May 31, but it was later extended to June 7.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> The second phase began on July 7, 2010, and ended on July 19, 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>In an interview held in June 2009, Rob Pardo indicated that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LAN" title="LAN">LAN</a> support would not be included in <i>StarCraft II</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Removing LAN requires players to connect through Blizzard's servers before being able to play multiplayer games, causing gamers to voice their dissatisfaction online.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-No_Lan_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-No_Lan-57">[57]</a></sup> Further controversy was sparked when Blizzard confirmed that the game would not support cross-server play out of the box, restricting gamers to only play against local opponents—for instance, US gamers against those in the US and Europeans against Europeans. The company originally explained that Australia and New Zealand servers would be located in Southeast Asia, pitting them against combatants from Indonesia, the Philippines, Malaysia, Singapore, and Thailand. However, starting from patch 1.1.0, it was announced that the Southeast Asia/Australia/New Zealand version of the game would not be strictly region-locked, with gamers able to play on both the SEA/ANZ servers and the North American ones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>Mike Morhaime, president of Blizzard, announced during the opening ceremony of Blizzcon 2009 that <i>StarCraft II</i> and the new Battle.net platform would both be released in 2010, with an approximately one-month gap between releases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> As of March 2010, Blizzard had stated that the new platform was being tested outside the beta and was planned for release in early July 2010,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> for both <i>StarCraft II</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/World_of_Warcraft:_Cataclysm" title="World of Warcraft: Cataclysm">World of Warcraft: Cataclysm</a></i>, with a later upgrade for <i><a href="/wiki/Diablo_III" title="Diablo III">Diablo III</a></i>. On May 5, 2010, it was revealed that <i>StarCraft II</i> and Battle.net 2.0 would be integrated with social networking site <a href="/wiki/Facebook" title="Facebook">Facebook</a>, "linking the world's premier online gaming platform with the world's most popular social platform"—a move that allowed gamers to search among their Facebook friends for <i>StarCraft II</i> opponents. <i><a href="/wiki/Wired_(magazine)" title="Wired (magazine)">Wired</a></i> magazine, in its annual <a href="/wiki/Vaporware" title="Vaporware">Vaporware</a> Award, ranked <i>StarCraft II</i> first among the delayed technology products of 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> <i>StarCraft II</i> was finally released on July 27, 2010,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> with launch parties in selected cities of countries around the world. Customers and reviewers who received the installation DVD before the July 27 release were greeted with an error screen, telling them to wait until the release date.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> There was no known workaround and some reviewers had praised the action for limiting journalists to playing and reviewing the game with actual players.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>Compared to the original <i>StarCraft</i>, <i>StarCraft II</i> was designed to focus more heavily on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multiplayer" title="Multiplayer">multiplayer</a> aspect, with changes that include major improvements to <a href="/wiki/Battle.net" title="Battle.net">Battle.net</a>, a new competitive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Game_ladder" title="Game ladder">"ladder" system</a> for ranked games, and new matchmaking mechanics that are designed to "match-up" players of equal skill level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-officialfaq_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-officialfaq-1">[1]</a></sup> In addition, the replay function, which allows players to record and review past games, was improved. Blizzard also stated that they incorporated changes to the game that were suggested by fans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-karune_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-karune-66">[66]</a></sup> <i>StarCraft II</i> continues its predecessor's use of pre-rendered cinematic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cut_scene" title="Cut scene">cut scenes</a> to advance the plot while also improving the quality of in-game cut scenes within the levels themselves, which are rendered on-the-fly using the same game engine as the graphics in the game proper. Blizzard stated that, with the new graphics engine that <i>StarCraft II</i> uses to render the gameplay, they "can actually create in-game cut-scenes of near-cinematic quality".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cutscenes_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-cutscenes-67">[67]</a></sup> Improvements include advanced scenery allocation and more detailed space terrain, such as floating space platforms with <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planets</a> and <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroids</a> in the background. Small cliffs, extensions, and even advertising signs were also shown to have been improved and refined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-video_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-video-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Expansions">Expansions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=StarCraft_II:_Wings_of_Liberty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Expansions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/StarCraft_II:_Heart_of_the_Swarm" title="StarCraft II: Heart of the Swarm">StarCraft II: Heart of the Swarm</a> and <a href="/wiki/StarCraft_II:_Legacy_of_the_Void" title="StarCraft II: Legacy of the Void">StarCraft II: Legacy of the Void</a></div>
<p>During the development of <i>StarCraft II</i>, it was also announced that the game and its expansions would form a trilogy, with each chapter to center on one of the three playable races. The first expansion – <i><a href="/wiki/StarCraft_II:_Heart_of_the_Swarm" title="StarCraft II: Heart of the Swarm">Heart of the Swarm</a></i>, which focuses on the Zerg race – was released on March 12, 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> The second expansion <i><a href="/wiki/StarCraft_II:_Legacy_of_the_Void" title="StarCraft II: Legacy of the Void">Legacy of the Void</a></i>, which centers on the Protoss race, was released on November 10, 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Release">Release</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=StarCraft_II:_Wings_of_Liberty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Release">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Versions">Versions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=StarCraft_II:_Wings_of_Liberty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Versions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On April 8, 2010, Blizzard officially announced that the game would be available in a standard and collector's edition. The game was also made available for digital download from Blizzard on the release date; pre-loading began on July 15.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> The collector's edition comes with an artbook, 2 GB flash drive modeled after <a href="/wiki/Jim_Raynor" title="Jim Raynor">Jim Raynor</a>'s dog tag with the original <i>StarCraft</i> and <i>Brood War</i> expansion preloaded, behind-the-scenes DVD, soundtrack, comic book, unique avatar portraits, a unique model for the in-game Thor unit in multiplayer, and a <i><a href="/wiki/World_of_Warcraft" title="World of Warcraft">World of Warcraft</a></i> pet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p>On June 24, 2010, at a press-only Korean event, Blizzard announced that Korean players would be able to play <i>StarCraft II</i> for free with an active <i>World of Warcraft</i> subscription. In <a href="/wiki/PC_bang" title="PC bang">PC bangs</a>, or other cybercafés, players can play the game by paying 500 to 1500 <a href="/wiki/South_Korean_won" title="South Korean won">South Korean won</a> (approx. $.50 to $1.50) per hour. Other options include a 30-day subscription for ₩9900 (approx. $8), a 24 hours play-time ticket for ₩2000 (approx. $1.50), and unlimited access for ₩69,000 (approx. $56).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Software_license_agreement" title="Software license agreement">end-user license agreement</a> (EULA) for <i>StarCraft II</i> differs significantly from those of Blizzard's earlier titles in that buying the game only grants the buyer a license to play, while the game itself remains the property of Blizzard. Any breach of the EULA amounts not only to breach of contract but copyright infringement as well, giving Blizzard greater control over how the game is used. Concerns have been raised by <a href="/wiki/Public_Knowledge" title="Public Knowledge">Public Knowledge</a> over how the altered EULA may affect multiplayer tournaments not endorsed by Blizzard.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-publicknowledge1_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-publicknowledge1-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>On August 3, 2011, Blizzard replaced the previously available <i>StarCraft II</i> demo with the new <i>StarCraft II: Starter Edition</i>. It allows anyone to play part of the game for free and it comes as a 7 GB package downloaded using the Blizzard Downloader client. The <i>Starter Edition</i> is available for Mac and Windows, and requires a Battle.net account and an Internet connection to play.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sales">Sales</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=StarCraft_II:_Wings_of_Liberty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Sales">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Starcraft_II_Commercial_on_Korean_Air_-_Seoul_Incheon_Airport_edit.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2736" data-file-width="3648" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Starcraft_II_Commercial_on_Korean_Air_-_Seoul_Incheon_Airport_edit.JPG/220px-Starcraft_II_Commercial_on_Korean_Air_-_Seoul_Incheon_Airport_edit.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Starcraft_II_Commercial_on_Korean_Air_-_Seoul_Incheon_Airport_edit.JPG/330px-Starcraft_II_Commercial_on_Korean_Air_-_Seoul_Incheon_Airport_edit.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Starcraft_II_Commercial_on_Korean_Air_-_Seoul_Incheon_Airport_edit.JPG/440px-Starcraft_II_Commercial_on_Korean_Air_-_Seoul_Incheon_Airport_edit.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Starcraft_II_Commercial_on_Korean_Air_-_Seoul_Incheon_Airport_edit.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Korean_Air" title="Korean Air">Korean Air</a> Boeing 747-400 at <a href="/wiki/Incheon_International_Airport" title="Incheon International Airport">Incheon International Airport</a> with an advertisement for <i>StarCraft II</i> painted on the fuselage. Jim Raynor is prominently displayed on the plane.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Blizzard entered into a co-marketing agreement with <a href="/wiki/Korean_Air" title="Korean Air">Korean Air</a> that lasted for six months, in which two of the airline's airplanes on both domestic and international routes prominently displayed <i>StarCraft II</i> advertising featuring Jim Raynor on the fuselage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> On August 3, 2010, Blizzard announced that <i>StarCraft II</i> sold more than one million units worldwide within one day of its release. After two days, when Blizzard began selling the game as a digital download on its website, approximately 500,000 additional units of the game were sold, bringing the total up to 1.5 million worldwide and making it the fastest-selling strategy game of all time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blizzard-sales_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blizzard-sales-76">[76]</a></sup> In its first month on sale, <i>StarCraft II</i> sold a total of three million copies worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3-million_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-3-million-9">[9]</a></sup> As of December 2010, the game has sold nearly 4.5 million units.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> The game was also heavily pirated, reportedly being downloaded over 2.3 million times, and setting a record for most data transferred by a single torrent in only three months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Torrent_statistics_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Torrent_statistics-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Technical_difficulties">Technical difficulties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=StarCraft_II:_Wings_of_Liberty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Technical difficulties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Several gaming and technology sites reported an "overheating bug" with <i>StarCraft II</i> that in some cases resulted in permanent damage to <a href="/wiki/Video_card" title="Video card">video cards</a>. The source of the problem is the fact that the frame rate is not locked on menu screens. This causes the graphics card to continuously render the image, resulting in excessive heat. Blizzard has acknowledged the problem, and posted a temporary workaround.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pcauthority.com.au_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-pcauthority.com.au-82">[82]</a></sup> They also recommended ensuring computer systems are well ventilated and contacting the videocard manufacturer for hardware-related issues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-techland.com_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-techland.com-83">[83]</a></sup> In response to the reports, Blizzard's Public Relations Manager, Bob Colayco said: "There is no code in our software that will cause video cards to overheat. When we saw this issue first reported, we conducted thorough additional testing and determined that for those players experiencing this problem, the cause is most likely hardware-related."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-techland.com_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-techland.com-83">[83]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CrunchGear" title="CrunchGear">CrunchGear</a> has also suggested that the problem is not with <i>StarCraft II</i>, but rather due to poorly maintained hardware and inadequate cooling. They do however agree that the overheating only occurs on non-framerate locked screens and provide the fix that Blizzard has offered to users.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Other articles recommend that users regularly clean the dust out of their computer to improve the cooling efficiency of their systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pcauthority.com.au_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-pcauthority.com.au-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> Blizzard posted a message on their official forums regarding the issue that has since been removed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pcauthority.com.au_82-2"><a href="#cite_note-pcauthority.com.au-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-blizzard-techissues_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-blizzard-techissues-86">[86]</a></sup> The original message was: "Screens that are light on detail may make your system overheat if cooling is overall insufficient. This is because the game has nothing to do so it is primarily just working on drawing the screen very quickly."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pcauthority.com.au_82-3"><a href="#cite_note-pcauthority.com.au-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-blizzard-techissues_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-blizzard-techissues-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reception">Reception</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=StarCraft_II:_Wings_of_Liberty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Reception">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<p><i>StarCraft II: Wings of Liberty</i> has been met with critical acclaim since its release. It received an aggregated score of 92.39% at <a href="/wiki/GameRankings" title="GameRankings">GameRankings</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gamerankings_score_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-gamerankings_score-87">[87]</a></sup> and 93/100 at <a href="/wiki/Metacritic" title="Metacritic">Metacritic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-metacritic_score_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-metacritic_score-88">[88]</a></sup> The game was particularly praised for retaining the popular RTS gameplay from <i>StarCraft</i>, while introducing new features and improved storytelling. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GamesRadar" title="GamesRadar">GamesRadar</a> felt that "in many ways, <i>StarCraft II: Wings of Liberty</i> feels like <i>StarCraft</i> 2.0 – and that’s a good thing", stating that it "delivers on all fronts".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gamesradarscore_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-gamesradarscore-95">[95]</a></sup> NZGamer.com said the game was "the best RTS game released in years and one of the best games on PC".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nzgamer_score_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-nzgamer_score-102">[102]</a></sup> In relation to its story, <a href="/wiki/GameTrailers" title="GameTrailers">GameTrailers</a> stated, "If there's anything immediately apparent from Wings of Liberty's story, it's that the series' narrative structure has evolved well beyond the original's sparse between-sortie intermissions," calling it "an epic and entertainingly told yarn",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gametrailers_score_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-gametrailers_score-96">[96]</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/Eurogamer" title="Eurogamer">Eurogamer</a> criticized the dialogue as being "flat" and the characters as being "either clichéd, banal or both".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eurog_score_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-eurog_score-90">[90]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Giant_Bomb" title="Giant Bomb">Giant Bomb</a> echoed this view while also noting the <i>Hyperion</i> portion between missions, finding it to have "more depth of character, more believable pathos, more surprise twists—than I honestly expected out of the story".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-giantbomb_score_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-giantbomb_score-97">[97]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/IGN" title="IGN">IGN</a> however noted that "no doubt franchise fans will eat it up, but newcomers may be wondering what all the fuss is about while going through the early missions that lack the kind of urgency you would hope when the fate of civilization is in peril."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ign_score_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-ign_score-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Joystiq" title="Joystiq">Joystiq</a> was very positive towards the improved multiplayer matchmaking service, calling it "similar to <a href="/wiki/Xbox_Live" title="Xbox Live">Xbox Live</a> and <a href="/wiki/PlayStation_Network" title="PlayStation Network">PlayStation Network</a>, which is a welcome change from the archaic matchmaking of Battle.net in previous Blizzard games",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-joystiq_score_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-joystiq_score-99">[99]</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/GameSpot" title="GameSpot">GameSpot</a> called the amount of online content "remarkable", noting the variety of maps and up to 12 player online support.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gamespot_score_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-gamespot_score-93">[93]</a></sup> When comparing the single and multiplayer modes, <a href="/wiki/GameSpy" title="GameSpy">GameSpy</a> felt that the single-player portion was "less inspiring, mostly because of the extremely shallow learning curve", with the online multiplayer being "so smooth, so challenging, and so much plain-old-fun".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gspy_score_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-gspy_score-94">[94]</a></sup> John Meyer of <i><a href="/wiki/Wired_(magazine)" title="Wired (magazine)">Wired</a></i> praised the improved graphics engine, saying that it "shows decades of polish" and a "slick new presentation".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> Matt Peckham of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PC_World_(magazine)" title="PC World (magazine)">PC World</a></i> also noted that some buyers expressed dissatisfaction with the absence of LAN-based multiplayer gameplay, the lack of cross-realm play and the campaign being limited to the Terran race.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Game_Revolution" title="Game Revolution">Game Revolution</a>, in relation to only being able to play the Terran campaign, however, pointed out that "<i>Wings of Liberty</i> has 29 missions; the original <i>StarCraft</i> had just over 30. Fair odds say the next one will have roughly the same amount; <i>Broodwar</i> brought about 30 too. We already got the full game for $50, and we’re getting offered two expansions. If you want to feel outraged about something, pick something else."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gamerev_score_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-gamerev_score-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ars_Technica" title="Ars Technica">Ars Technica</a> gave the overall game a verdict of "buy" and especially praised the single-player campaign as "fun as hell". They were also very impressed with the game modding tools that allow users to create their own content and distribute it to other fans. The two "ugly" issues they identified with the game were lack of LAN play and the decision to split up the regions. They suggested that these decisions were influenced by <a href="/wiki/Activision" title="Activision">Activision</a> rather than by the game designers at Blizzard, and felt that this would turn many people off of the game and make things difficult for people who have international friends.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> In a separate article, Ars Technica vigorously supported <i>StarCraft II</i> against a series of 1-star ratings given to the game on Amazon.com in protest against the lack of LAN play.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> They argued in Blizzard's defense against complaints that it was not a full game because only the Terran campaign was released, but did suggest that the customers' complaints about the lack of LAN and cross-region play were legitimate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup></p>
<p>In August 2016, <i>StarCraft II: Wings of Liberty</i> placed 26th on <a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time</a>'s The 50 Best Video Games of All Time list.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Soundtracks">Soundtracks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=StarCraft_II:_Wings_of_Liberty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Soundtracks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="infobox vevent haudio" style="width:22em">
<tr>
<th class="summary album" colspan="2" style="text-align:center;font-size:125%;font-weight:bold;font-style: italic; background-color: gainsboro">StarCraft II: Wings of Liberty Soundtrack</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<th class="description" colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background-color: gainsboro"><a href="/wiki/Soundtrack_album" title="Soundtrack album">Soundtrack album</a> by <span class="contributor">Russell Brower, Glenn Stafford, Derek Duke, Neal Acree, Sascha Dikiciyan &amp; Cris Velasco</span></th>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Released</th>
<td class="published">July 27, 2010<span style="display:none"> (<span class="bday dtstart published updated">2010-07-27</span>)</span></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Music_genre" title="Music genre">Genre</a></th>
<td class="category"><a href="/wiki/Video_game_music" title="Video game music">Video game soundtrack</a></td>

<th scope="row">Length</th>
<td>67:12</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Record_label" title="Record label">Label</a></th>
<td>Azeroth Music</td>

<p>Two official soundtracks were released for <i>StarCraft II: Wings of Liberty</i>. The original <a href="/wiki/Film_score" title="Film score">score</a>, composed by Derek Duke, Glenn Stafford, Neal Acree, Russell Brower, Sascha Dikiciyan and Cris Velasco was released on CD as well as on the <a href="/wiki/ITunes_Store" title="ITunes Store">iTunes Store</a>. Both versions contain 14 tracks and the iTunes version contains additional digital extras.</p>
<p>A second soundtrack, <i><a href="/wiki/Revolution_Overdrive:_Songs_of_Liberty" title="Revolution Overdrive: Songs of Liberty">Revolution Overdrive: Songs of Liberty</a></i>, was released on CD, vinyl and iTunes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> This soundtrack features the original and cover songs heard in JoeyRay's bar during the video game.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Professional_competition">Professional competition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=StarCraft_II:_Wings_of_Liberty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Professional competition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Professional_StarCraft_competition" title="Professional StarCraft competition">Professional StarCraft competition</a></div>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/StarCraft:_Brood_War" title="StarCraft: Brood War">StarCraft: Brood War</a></i> has been called the most successful <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/E-sport" title="E-sport">e-sport</a> in the world that includes its own ranking system, and it has been referred to as the national pastime in South Korea, where there are two television channels dedicated to broadcasting professional <i>StarCraft</i> matches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> Since its launch, <i>StarCraft II</i> has become a successful <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electronic_sports" title="Electronic sports">e-Sport</a> with many leagues of ongoing tournaments with prize-pools up to US$170,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> There have been many tournaments with players from around the world; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GOMTV_Global_StarCraft_II_League" title="GOMTV Global StarCraft II League">GomTV SC2 Global League</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TeamLiquid" title="TeamLiquid">TeamLiquid</a> StarCraft League,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Gaming" title="Major League Gaming">Major League Gaming</a>, ESL<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> and the North American Star League.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> <i>StarCraft II</i> has also become the main title in major LANs throughout the world like Assembly<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dreamhack" title="Dreamhack">Dreamhack</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup></p>
<p>The first large <i>StarCraft II</i> tournaments occurred during the beta testing phase, the most notable being the HDH Invitational<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Sean_Plott" title="Sean Plott">Day[9]</a>'s King of the Beta.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> Currently the largest professional tournament is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GOMTV_Global_StarCraft_II_League" title="GOMTV Global StarCraft II League">GSL (GomTV Star League)</a> which is a monthly tournament held in South Korea. The tournament enjoys major corporate sponsorships and prize pools of over $100,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> A new North American StarCraft League was announced on February 21, 2011 and is scheduled for an initial run of three seasons offering $400,000 in prize money.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> The first season of the North American Star League started on April 12, 2011, with 50 players divided into 5 divisions. It ended on July 10, 2011, and the total prize pool was US$100,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> The North American Star League has since run for an additional season, with a fifth starting in early 2013. On May 2, 2012, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ongamenet" title="Ongamenet">Ongamenet</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/KeSPA" title="KeSPA">KeSPA</a> announced a formal switch in <i>StarCraft</i> competition from <i>Brood War</i> to <i>StarCraft II: Wings of Liberty</i>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Starleague_(Ongamenet)" title="Starleague (Ongamenet)">Starleague</a> will transition over to <i>StarCraft II</i> by late 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-liquid_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-liquid-124">[124]</a></sup></p>
<p>After three years of negotiations, Blizzard decided that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Korean_e-Sports_Players_Association" title="Korean e-Sports Players Association">Korean e-Sports Players Association</a> (KeSPA) was unwilling to cooperate with them in regards to the sharing of profits from competitive <i>StarCraft</i> multiplayer games. This breakdown led to an uncertain future of KeSPA's legal ability to broadcast Blizzard's <a href="/wiki/Intellectual_property" title="Intellectual property">intellectual property</a> of both <i>StarCraft</i> and <i>StarCraft II</i> without paying royalties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-publicknowledge1_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-publicknowledge1-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yeskygaming_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-yeskygaming-125">[125]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MBC_Television" title="MBC Television">MBC Television</a> agreed to Blizzard's new terms in June 2010;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newhua_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-newhua-126">[126]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/KeSPA" title="KeSPA">KeSPA</a> received a license from Blizzard<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (August 2012)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ongamenet" title="Ongamenet">Ongamenet</a> will host <i>StarCraft II</i> competitions starting in the summer of 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-liquid_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-liquid-124">[124]</a></sup></p>
<p>Blizzard and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GomTV" title="GomTV">GomTV</a> signed an agreement on May 26, 2010, allowing the latter to create and broadcast the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GOMTV_Global_StarCraft_II_League" title="GOMTV Global StarCraft II League">GomTV SC2 Global League</a>, a series of tournaments, each with a US$170,000 prize pool, in South Korea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-teamLiquid_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-teamLiquid-127">[127]</a></sup> This agreement followed the decision from Blizzard to cease the negotiation with KeSPA, and it confirmed that Blizzard had decided to take another route to promote <i>StarCraft II</i> as an e-Sport in South Korea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-publicknowledge1_73-2"><a href="#cite_note-publicknowledge1-73">[73]</a></sup> The non-profit public interest group <a href="/wiki/Public_Knowledge" title="Public Knowledge">Public Knowledge</a> made the following statement regarding the issue: "The Battle.net Terms of Use state that it is a violation of the agreement—and an infringement of Blizzard's copyright in the underlying game—to "use the Service for any 'e-sports' or group competition sponsored, promoted or facilitated by any commercial or non-profit entity without Blizzard's prior written consent."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-publicknowledge1_73-3"><a href="#cite_note-publicknowledge1-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Battle.net_World_Championship_Series">Battle.net World Championship Series</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=StarCraft_II:_Wings_of_Liberty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Battle.net World Championship Series">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Battle.net_World_Championship_Series" title="Battle.net World Championship Series">Battle.net World Championship Series</a></div>
<p>In early 2012 <a href="/wiki/Blizzard_Entertainment" title="Blizzard Entertainment">Blizzard Entertainment</a> announced the <a href="/wiki/2012_StarCraft_II_World_Championship_Series" title="2012 StarCraft II World Championship Series">2012 StarCraft II World Championship Series</a> (WCS), featuring over 30 offline events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> The WCS featured thousands of dollars in prize pools and a system starting from National Qualifiers, National Finals, Continental Finals, and culminating in the World Championship Finals in Shanghai, China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> The Battle.net World Championship (also known as the WCS Global Finals) winner was professional South Korean player <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lee-Sak_%22PartinG%22_Won" title='Lee-Sak "PartinG" Won'>Lee-Sak "PartinG" Won</a> from team StarTale, winning both the trophy and US$100,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup></p>
<p>There are other StarCraft 2 tournaments that may not bear the WCS name, but allow players to earn WCS points. A few such tournaments include some of the aforementioned tournaments such as <a href="/wiki/Intel_Extreme_Masters" title="Intel Extreme Masters">Intel Extreme Masters</a> (IEM) and <a href="/wiki/DreamHack" title="DreamHack">DreamHack</a> Open, but also ASUS ROG,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> Red Bull Battlegrounds,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> and Homestory Cup.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> A full list of tournaments can be found on the StarCraft 2 Liquidpedia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup></p>
<p>Professional <i>StarCraft II</i> began to decline in popularity by 2012, in part because of competition from games like <i><a href="/wiki/League_of_Legends" title="League of Legends">League of Legends</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="LAN_play">LAN play</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=StarCraft_II:_Wings_of_Liberty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: LAN play">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><i>StarCraft II</i> does not offer the ability to play directly over a <a href="/wiki/Local_area_network" title="Local area network">local area network</a> (LAN), as is possible with <i>StarCraft</i>; all network games are routed through the Internet via Blizzard's gaming servers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Latency_(engineering)" title="Latency (engineering)">latency</a> delay between commands issued and game response when played online is greatly reduced when playing over a LAN and this allows for much finer control over in-game units;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mlg-dallas_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-mlg-dallas-137">[137]</a></sup> there were concerns that a professional scene would not develop as a result.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> Over 250,000 fans signed a petition asking Blizzard to add LAN play to <i>StarCraft II</i>, before the game's release.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> Currently, Blizzard has no plans to support LAN play.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> Although it does not change the routing of the game through Blizzard's servers, a <i>Players Near You</i> feature was added in <i>StarCraft II</i> patch 2.0.4, to help with organizing games with other players on the same local network.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup></p>
<p>Lack of LAN play caused problems in 2010 <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Gaming" title="Major League Gaming">Major League Gaming</a> tournament in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dallas,_Texas" title="Dallas, Texas">Dallas, Texas</a>, which experienced severe delays and problems with Battle.net 2.0.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mlg-dallas_137-1"><a href="#cite_note-mlg-dallas-137">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jinro-wins_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-jinro-wins-142">[142]</a></sup></p>
